              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=pure-power-of-gr">pure-power-of-gr</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=4-star">4-star</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=reason-for-history-class">reason-for-history-class</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=person-of-everything">person-of-everything</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer725085790" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11945975614364050541"><blockquote>
  <i>Words can be practically useless at times, but as a writer they're all I have.</i>
</blockquote>A group of people killed another group of people. I could be talking about a number of things. A description of the present. An incident in history. A prediction of the future. The final result of brainwashing. The beginning of a new era. A cause. A conclusion. The means. The end. A resolution. An excuse. The threat executed by an oppressor. The exigency fulfilled by a nation. Culture clash. Revolution. Murder. Sel</span>
  <span id="freeText11945975614364050541" style="display:none"><blockquote>
  <i>Words can be practically useless at times, but as a writer they're all I have.</i>
</blockquote>A group of people killed another group of people. I could be talking about a number of things. A description of the present. An incident in history. A prediction of the future. The final result of brainwashing. The beginning of a new era. A cause. A conclusion. The means. The end. A resolution. An excuse. The threat executed by an oppressor. The exigency fulfilled by a nation. Culture clash. Revolution. Murder. Self-defense. Genocide. The right to bear arms. The death penalty. Patriotism. Nationalism. Faith. Religion. Heaven. Hell. Pick a cult, pick a cult: any cult'll do.<blockquote>
  <i>[I]f someone had fallen down right in front of me, I like to think I'd have helped. But what if they fell 50 yards away? Would I go out of my way to help? I wonder. I might have seen it as somebody else's business and walked on by. If I'd got involved I'd have been late for work...</i>
</blockquote>Japan isn't the United States, the country my country where shootings have indoctrinated the public with the regularity of the weather. One of the interviewees who had been gassed by sarin hypothesized that, had the same incident occurred in a public transport on US soil, every passenger would have been gung ho from the get go and reacted to the incident much more effectively. Perhaps, but that would've depended on the relative level of segregation of the public transport, the social net of the state within which the public transport was contained, the standard deviation of care across the board of passengers under the capitalistic gutting that is the US healthcare system. Fire in a crowded theater, more like. And, of course, the one execution of function the United States is an absolute master of that would vent the fallout of an incident like the Tokyo Gas Attack across decades of reaction: the scapegoat.<blockquote>
  <i>Haven't you offered up some part of your Self to someone (or some thing), and taken on a "narrative" in return? Haven't we entrusted some par of our personality to some greater System or Order?</i>
</blockquote>I recently caught up with a friend who's been stuck at an underpaid occupation for some time. It was manageable when concurrent with high school and somewhat excusable when conducted alongside college, but now that my friend is out and about with a degree and all, lies involving "experience" and "exposure" and less than living wage don't cut it when financial independence is a simultaneous expectation. Trust In This System And The System Will Reward, and you don't have to renounce the secular life entirely or follow the murderous orders of a cult to do it. The desk job and the standard hours and the cold hard cash may distance and disillusion, but see, I've been part of a genocidal military industrial complex since day one of my existence through no execution of will of my own, so forgive me if my priorities are a compromise of my awareness and my livelihood. There's nothing that saps the strength of continued existence more than the pile up of the little hypocrisies on top of the huge and terrifying paradoxes, so something like Aum doesn't baffle while the Navy advertises its global map of bases before Pixar movies.<blockquote>
  <i>Later, when the police asked me "Didn't people start to panic?" I thought back on it: "Everyone was so silent. No one uttered word."</i>
</blockquote>It's the same old common denominator of death drawing together the dying and the death-dealing once again in a somewhat different environment using somewhat different tools and ideological modus operandi. One thing that's different is I can let myself trust a little more the truth I'm hearing, cause if I have to read another white person talking as if they know anything about non-white people and getting paid for it before I'm dead and gone, it'll be too soon. Another is that I've gotten over my whole anti-religion fad after years of being told on a theological level morality was where it wasn't and wasn't where it was, so I don't stop at Aum as the ultimate mystery and instead continue on to the government, and the corporate work force, and the fact that a nation not giving a fuck about certain groups of its people will always have consequences. Yet another is Murakami not screwing up his intentions too much, cause while it'd be absurd to believe any of these interviews are candid, the sheer number of instances of Japanese people talking to Japanese people from both sides of a common event results in as close to an honest text as can be achieved in this global age of ours. Both the author and those he interviews throw around the words "crazy" and "insane" too much, and there's a moment of disability inspiration porn that's just plain wrong, but if Murakami wasn't disturbed by how much the sarin victims mirrored the former and not so former Aum members in terms of certain thoughts and feelings to the point of questioning it to this day, I'll eat my hat.<blockquote>
  <i>If there's someone looking ill on the train I always say, "Are you okay? Want to sit down?" But not most people — I really learned that the hard way.</i>
</blockquote>You can't come together and stay together and act all surprised when the actions of the group reflect on the individual in such a way that is completely different from the side of the story you were initially drawn in through. All that matters is the means by which you trade in the blood on your hands for peace of mind. You don't need religious fanaticism for that.<blockquote>
  <i>I came to them from the "safety zone", someone who could always walk away whenever I wanted. Had they told me "There's no way you can truly know what we feel," I'd have had to agree. End of story.</i>
</blockquote></span>

<div id="review_2146226214" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2146226214" />
    <a title="Katherine Addison" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/36603555-katherine-addison"><img alt="Katherine Addison" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1415129731p2/36603555.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2146226214?book_show_action=true">Oct 07, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Katherine Addison" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Katherine Addison" href="/user/show/36603555-katherine-addison">Katherine Addison</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780375725807" href="/book/show/229173.Underground">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36603555-katherine-addison?shelf=aum-shinrikyo">aum-shinrikyo</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36603555-katherine-addison?shelf=20th-century">20th-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36603555-katherine-addison?shelf=library">library</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36603555-katherine-addison?shelf=tokyo-japan-1995">tokyo-japan-1995</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36603555-katherine-addison?shelf=quest-object">quest-object</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36603555-katherine-addison?shelf=true-crime">true-crime</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2146226214" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1930026241524079382">[library]<br/><br/>This is a book about the sarin attack in the Tokyo subway system on March 20, 1995. As you would expect from Murakami, it is thoughtful and careful. It consists primarily of interviews; the first part, <em>Underground</em>, is interviews with survivors; the second part, <em>The Place that was Promised</em>, is interviews with members and former members of Aum Shinrikyo, the cult responsible for the attacks.<br/><br/> The translation, insofar as I can judge, seems good. I hope it's accurate. The one thing I would h</span>
  <span id="freeText1930026241524079382" style="display:none">[library]<br /><br />This is a book about the sarin attack in the Tokyo subway system on March 20, 1995. As you would expect from Murakami, it is thoughtful and careful. It consists primarily of interviews; the first part, <em>Underground</em>, is interviews with survivors; the second part, <em>The Place that was Promised</em>, is interviews with members and former members of Aum Shinrikyo, the cult responsible for the attacks.<br /><br /> The translation, insofar as I can judge, seems good. I hope it's accurate. The one thing I would have liked was for more Japanese words to be included along with their translations--there are several words that get used frequently by the interview subjects that I would like to have the original word for, simply because they seem so important. That's a quibble, not a real defect in the translation, and is probably highly idiosyncratic. <br /><br />I have two reactions to this book. One is the writer's reaction to everything, including their own personal disasters of overwhelming magnitudes: <em>how can I use this in a story?</em> It always feels like a callous and insensitive reaction, and I'm generally reluctant to articulate it for that reason, but it's actually the opposite, because it's asking, <em>how can I take this experience into myself and turn it into something I can send out again that other people can read and understand and empathize with?</em> Which insofar as I'm willing to argue for a higher purpose to storytelling (aside from just, <em>people need stories to stay alive and sane</em>), is the higher purpose I would argue for. So, yeah, on that level, this is a great book for worldbuilding writers to read to understand the depth and complexity that our worldbuilding most often lacks. I say this not to equate modern Japan with an imaginary country, but to say that our imaginary countries need to aspire to be as <strong>deep</strong> as Japan is in Murakami's work here (yes, that's a pun; yes, I meant it). If art is going to imitate life, it needs to do so consciously and carefully and with the best understanding we can bring to the table of what we're trying to imitate. And it works better if we read about a city we <strong>don't</strong> know so that our own familiarity doesn't blind us to how much information is being presented. I don't know a great deal about Japanese society in general, or the city of Tokyo in particular, so this was a window on an alien world for me (using "alien" in the Ruth-amidst-the-alien-corn sense, not the aliens-from-outer-space sense), and I found it fascinating. As someone who's trying right now to invent a city that has a true, deep, four-dimensional presence in my narrative, this was a kind of textbook of all the things I need to think about and understand to make that kind of verisimilitude possible.<br /><br />This book was an amazing worldbuilding demonstration, though, <strong>precisely because</strong> Murakami isn't worldbuilding at all. He's telling us (and here I mean "us" as both Japanese readers and the Anglophone readers of the translation) about a real place and real people and a real tragedy that happened in 1995. He's not trying to present 1995 Tokyo to readers of a novel; he's trying to come to grips with a terrible event in his own life and his country's life.<br /><br />(This is why you should occasionally branch out into nonfiction, even if you write fantasy, so that you get Tokyo, for example, as it was in 1995, not Tokyo as filtered through a writer's imagination and tailored to the needs of a story.)<br /><br />And that's where the writer's reaction blends into the reader's reaction, which is that this is a fascinating book about a terrible tragedy. Murakami admits he's an amateur interviewer, which he makes up for by being patient and thorough and manifestly a good listener. The survivors' accounts are vivid and telling: they capture how tragedy enters your life without your suspecting its existence and how it alienates you from your own life. Also how <strong>confusing</strong> a disaster is as it unfolds. (Which is a pretty good practical takeaway, if you need one.)<br /><br />The Aum Shinrikyo interviews are equally fascinating, partly because of the way that Aum's Cloud Cuckooland morphed from utopia to dystopia with almost no one even noticing. None of Murakami's interviewees had <strong>ANY IDEA</strong> that their leadership was planning to plant packets of sarin in the Tokyo subway (and I do in fact believe that they did not know). These people, both those who remain in Aum and those who have left it, are almost all absolutely baffled at how something that was so meaningful and valuable to them could possibly have resulted in the calculated monstrosity of the sarin attack. (A very few of them saw Mr Hyde behind Aum's beaming Jekyll-Buddha face before 1995 03 20, but even they didn't know the scope of the rottenness at Aum's core, and they clearly can't find a story that makes sense of it.) So on the one hand, there's a very serious discussion about Buddhism and asceticism and the perceived brokenness of Japanese culture that carries through several of the interviews; on a second hand there's a patchwork description of the inner workings of a cult; and on a third hand there are a series of intensely personal discussions about belief and disillusionment. Murakami captures snapshots of people at every point along that progression, from those who are still trying to salvage Aum Shinrikyo as a movement to those who have denounced it along with its founder. All of them are struggling to reintegrate in any way at all with the world outside Aum, especially given the police persecution they were continuing to suffer in 1997 &amp; 1998 when Murakami was doing his interviews. (Every time they find jobs, the police come along and tell their employers about their affiliation with Aum, and bang! there they are out of a job again. At least in 1998, they were remaining very insular, mostly existing in a bootstrap economy with other outcasts and refugees from Aum.) <br /><br />Murakami's sections of meta, about the interview process and his own experience, are thoughtful and compassionate. He can't make sense of what happened, any more than the survivors and the members and ex-members of Aum can, but I feel like this book does succeed in framing the mystery at its center so that we understand the dimensions of the thing that we cannot comprehend.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1930026241524079382" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2265159438" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2265159438" />
    <a title="Widyanto Gunadi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/49008614-widyanto-gunadi"><img alt="Widyanto Gunadi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1604538989p2/49008614.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2265159438?book_show_action=true">Feb 08, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Widyanto Gunadi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Widyanto Gunadi" href="/user/show/49008614-widyanto-gunadi">Widyanto Gunadi</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780375703881" href="/book/show/15225517-underground">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2265159438" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9737095705883759985">Haruki Murakami's journalistic effort in recording a daring and controversial subject in relation to the hazardous Japanese cult organization led by Aum, an esoteric figure behind Aum Shinrikyo is a masterfully written, heartrending book. In this nonfiction work, Murakami-san has interviewed several victims of the nationally shocking, chemical-fueled terrorist attack carried out in a Tokyo subway train station, back in 1995. He gave us, readers, fairly neutral point of view and coverage of the w</span>
  <span id="freeText9737095705883759985" style="display:none">Haruki Murakami's journalistic effort in recording a daring and controversial subject in relation to the hazardous Japanese cult organization led by Aum, an esoteric figure behind Aum Shinrikyo is a masterfully written, heartrending book. In this nonfiction work, Murakami-san has interviewed several victims of the nationally shocking, chemical-fueled terrorist attack carried out in a Tokyo subway train station, back in 1995. He gave us, readers, fairly neutral point of view and coverage of the whole topic, derivatively taken from all of these truthful and meticulously accurate conversational accounts with the survivors of the horrendous accident as well as the former members of the mysterious religious sorority. Upon a complete reading of this journalism literature, we get to see some pivotal, requisite facets of Japanese conscience and psyche. As a society with quite a fanatic obsession for work and a high rate of suicides resulted from acute depression, no wonder that some Japanese people are always in search of a higher calling and meanings behind their seemingly pointless and repetitively monotonous, boring everyday existence. Thus, in the pilgrimage to find a spiritual comfort to escape the unbearable constant boredom which befell them, ethereal concepts such as one offered by Aum Shinrikyo, has gathered quite a large number of devout followers without knowing of the true intent of its notorious leader, which was later revealed, as to led them astray into doing a nationwide infamously condemned criminal charade. This book has an urgently potent transformative power to challenge and change the stereotypical worldviews commonly shared by many casually knowledgable visitors of Japan, as it will expose you to one of the Land of the Rising Sun's undercover hidden truths. Conclusively, if you so loved Japan and its culture, and wanted to read something so thrillingly thoughtful, albeit heartwrenching timeline, of the East Asian country, do pick up Underground at your nearest local bookstore.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9737095705883759985" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_315176282" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/315176282" />
    <a title="David" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6971227-david"><img alt="David" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1564001692p2/6971227.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/315176282?book_show_action=true">Apr 19, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David" href="/user/show/6971227-david">David</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780375725807" href="/book/show/229173.Underground">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6971227-david?shelf=asian">asian</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer315176282" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11661755408148564709"><i>
  <blockquote>The rain that fell on the city runs down the dark gutters and empties into the sea without even soaking the ground.</blockquote>
</i>The sarin gas attacks on the Tokyo subway in 1995 were perpetrated by Aum Shinrikyo, a Japanese religious cult, and attracted wide media attention. Aum emerged in 1984 when previously strict measures by the Japanese government against new religions were relaxed. During the post-war American occupation and in the years following, this laxness was an attempt to show that the new p</span>
  <span id="freeText11661755408148564709" style="display:none"><i>
  <blockquote>The rain that fell on the city runs down the dark gutters and empties into the sea without even soaking the ground.</blockquote>
</i>The sarin gas attacks on the Tokyo subway in 1995 were perpetrated by Aum Shinrikyo, a Japanese religious cult, and attracted wide media attention. Aum emerged in 1984 when previously strict measures by the Japanese government against new religions were relaxed. During the post-war American occupation and in the years following, this laxness was an attempt to show that the new political regime in Japan was pursuing progress toward religious freedom. The group’s leader, Asahara Shoko, envisioned himself as a “Lamb of God” and prophesized a doomsday that culminated in a nuclear Armageddon between the United States and Asia. Cult members included a significant number of young scientists, engineers and young students who were drawn in by Asahara’s offering of an alternative to rigid Japanese conformity. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/785200.Religion_in_Contemporary_Japan" rel="nofollow noopener">Ian Reader</a> points out that young people were attracted by the media’s portrayal of Asahara as “an embittered youth at war with Japanese society from early on.” Despite some media attention in years prior to the attacks, the group gained notoriety following the subway attack in 1995. Membership had reached an estimated 10,000 in Japan and an equal amount in Russia.<br /> <br />The March 20, 1995 attacks killed eleven people and forced 5,500 others to seek medical attention; the attack is the largest incident of terror on Japanese soil in its history. The attack was launched during peak rush hour to maximize exposure, with five members releasing sarin gas at predetermined station locations on the Chiyoda, Marunouchi and Hibiya lines. Once punctured, the bags containing the sarin gas permeated the subway cars and stations, affecting passengers, subway employees and those with whom affected people came in contact. Given the large scale of the attack, the Japanese government demanded information about the attacks and the media gladly provided it – however, the story that the media propagated may have been divergent from the true facts about the cult and about the attacks. Furthermore, the media continued to run Aum-related stories through July; the first day that there was no Aum-related story in Asahi newspaper was 114 days after the event. State-owned NHK and private networks ran Aum-related stories thought August. According to broadcasters’ statistics, from March 20, 1995 until mid-June, more than 500 media hours were devoted to Aum, outstripping even the wedding of the crown prince (419 hours), and viewer rates topped all other topics. These statistics, taken with the media framing, exemplify the media’s potential pandering of stories to Japanese society’s appetite for news on the “atypical” and sensational stories of the Aum cult members and the subway attacks.<br /><br />(The above is from a term paper I wrote for a freshman seminar. For the full dose of laudanum, the paper is located <a href="https://bu.digication.com/davidlavieri_portfolio/TITLE" rel="nofollow noopener">here</a>.)<br /><br />Though his surreal/fantastic fiction has earned him international celebrity (and, what I believe are undeserved, pleas for a Nobel award), Haruki Murakami proves to be a talented non-fiction writer and a keen interviewer in his collection <i>Underground</i> which chronicles the life changing effects of those affected, the experiences of unsuspecting spectators, and the dark psychological catacombs of the Aum followers themselves. What is most striking in this collection is the deadening routine of Japanese society: a society whose somnabulating routine brought it over the very precipice of this disaster. Post-war Japan became a nation of sleepwalkers, stuck between the international pressure towards self-loathing, and the national fervor for rejuvenation and rebirth. The society, which in the 1980s and 1990s was swept up in an economic boom became increasingly fixated on efficiency: business efficiency begot moral expediency and the rigid cage of social conformism. Aum won over many young intellectuals (students, engineers) who felt alienated by a strict and severe culture which ceremoniously cast out those who failed to toe the line of propriety and conformity.<br /><br />Murakami's interviews reveal an alarming composure and similarity between the terrorized and the agents of terror. Many's lives were changed forever, families broken up, financial pressure increased, jobs lost, but the stories of the terrorists are surprisingly stirring. Both sides of this story are moving. Despite the villainy of the Aum cultists, they are portrayed here as humans, flawed and outcast, blindly following a demogogue who made them feel included. Through the two sections of this volume, Murakami casts the shadow of blame across the whole country of Japan, which has lost its humanity in the strident effort to mechanize and automate. In a country where the train arrivals and departures are determined to the hundredth of a second, the well of human compassion has gone untended and dried alarmingly low. A media obsessed with sensation largely ignores the fates of victims: slummed silhouettes on the cement citadel of the humming Japanese econocracy. Murakami seeks the truth: truth in the terrorized and truth in the terrorists, and finds a truth central to a country which has worn thin its moral fiber and spent its currency of compassion.<br /><br />Like many modern societies, the narrative of Murakami's Japan, in <i>Underground</i> as well as his body of fiction, is a society of the alienated Self. The economic-fervor and moral no-mans-land of post-war Japan becomes weighted battle between the individual self and the over-industrialized economic machine of Modern Japan. A landscape idealized as sloping hills, patchwork rice-paddies, torii gates and Buddhist temples, trickling rivulets and the red rising-sun over the spectral face of Mt. Fuji: the ever pale-purple peak on the horizon, has become a cement-gray prison of daily routine, social conservatism and creative suppression. The boom of metropolitan Tokyo, the explosion of the export economy: all things were cast in the shadow of Yen. But more than a profit-hungry monster, Japanese society became a different sort of monstrosity: one which became complicit with its own disaster, a compliant slave to subway schedules and time-tables. Not the profit-motive, but the straight-and-narrow. Spiritual decline, alienation for society, demanding schedules which ate away most of everyday became the ostensibly innocuous venoms which fed the lurking monster of terror in the shadows of industrialized and modernized Japan life. Murakami unmasks victim and villain with a balance of distance and human compassion, which reveals both the intricate threads of individual lives: the warp and woof, minute and measure of the <i>humans</i> of March 20, 1995, rather than the media-embellished shadow puppets of evil and their unsuspecting doe-eyed innocents, as well as the torn tapestry of a nation in trouble.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11661755408148564709" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_126826787" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/126826787" />
    <a title="Seth T." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/160319-seth-t"><img alt="Seth T." src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1251915636p2/160319.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/126826787?book_show_action=true">Oct 18, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Seth T." class="user" itemprop="url" name="Seth T." href="/user/show/160319-seth-t">Seth T.</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780375725807" href="/book/show/229173.Underground">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer126826787" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4411232313546913446">Murakami's <i>Underground</i> was by turns devastating and intriguing. There were moments I wanted to abandon humanity in a wastebin behind an abortion clinic and others when I sat there dumbfounded, thinking <i>Wow, humanity, you're like the most interesting people on earth.</i> Love to hate to love to hate. Again and again.<br/><br/>That's what books about patent insanities do to me.<br/><br/><i>Underground</i> chronicles the psychological aftermath of Aum Shinrikyo's 1995 deposit of Sarin nerve gas across several of the mass-transit</span>
  <span id="freeText4411232313546913446" style="display:none">Murakami's <i>Underground</i> was by turns devastating and intriguing. There were moments I wanted to abandon humanity in a wastebin behind an abortion clinic and others when I sat there dumbfounded, thinking <i>Wow, humanity, you're like the most interesting people on earth.</i> Love to hate to love to hate. Again and again.<br /><br />That's what books about patent insanities do to me.<br /><br /><i>Underground</i> chronicles the psychological aftermath of Aum Shinrikyo's 1995 deposit of Sarin nerve gas across several of the mass-transit trains that service the city of Tokyo. It does so primarily by way of interview with victims of the attack (and at times, family members of victims), followed both by an extended essay by Murakami himself and a post-published epilogue of interviews with members and former members of the Aum cult (none of whom were involved in or even aware of the attacks until much later).<br /><br />Honestly, I found myself pretty well enthralled by each discreet portion of the book despite the fact they may as well have been their own separate books. There's little thematic crossover between the three parts beyond the obvious shared subject matter, so the reader must change gears as each section bears down to become the new asphalt of the road Murakami presents.<br /><br />In the first section, we feel anger and fury and pain and anguish and maybe even a little terror as we read firsthand accounts of train passengers who gradually succumbed to the Sarin's effects. Murakami keeps each of his interviews in roughly identical form beginning with a healthy bit of background on the interviewee, so that we can get to know our protagonists before we watch them fall to the real-life effects of religious madness. Then subjects generally recall the day before the attack and follow up with the morning's events as they unfolded to their own particular perspective. Finally, each individual speaks about any lingering effects from the attack, whether physical or psychological, and Murakami caps the interview by asking after the victim's emotional response to Aum, to the government, to Japan. While the descriptions of the attack itself are certainly harrowing, I found the emotional response to Aum the most intriguing. Some individuals are furious (some of these going so far as to say that they would participate in the execution of Shoko Asahara if the opportunity presented itself). Some are beyond anger and strike the reader as just dynamically confused by the whole event. Others are far more detached, saying things like how they do not approve of Aum's actions but they don't understand how anger can solve anything now.<br /><br />In the second section, Murakami takes us from knee-deep involvement to an almost wholly detached treatment, a kind of autopsy of both Aum's actions and Japan's inability to react to it in any helpful way. This section was well-proposed but would have been better received in the original work, functioning as a bit of epilogue to the whole endeavor. As it is, Murakami's words are sandwiched between the tragedy of the victims and the eerie logic of the cultists and so is kind of overwhelmed and, so, muffled.<br /><br />The final section features The Other Side. Mostly. Almost to a person, the interviews in this section denounce outright the use of Sarin against the civilian population, so we don't get to hear from any of those who actually carried out Asahara's orders. Still, this final third of the book offers some powerful insights into the minds of those who would join an apocalyptic Buddhist splinter-group that would move from yoga-like practices and asceticism to hosting a militaristic, hunted paradigm. Murakami's questioning of these cult members is valuable because it demonstrates how adherence to any system of belief, whether fringe cultic, mainstream religion, or simply secular ideology can lead individuals into difficult and dangerous places.<br /><br />[note: I should also express my disappointment upon discovering that a good two-thirds of the victim interviews were cut for the American release. Disappointing and anger-making. As if someone consciously decided that those victims' pain was not great enough to be valuable to an American audience.]</span>
  <a data-text-id="4411232313546913446" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/787183351?book_show_action=true">Dec 11, 2013</a>

          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780375703881" href="/book/show/15225517-underground">review of another edition</a>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2963923-gitte---bookworm-s-closet?shelf=japanese">japanese</a>,

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2963923-gitte---bookworm-s-closet?shelf=2015">2015</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer787183351" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6843746385621380418"><a href='http://bookwormscloset.com/underground/' rel='nofollow noopener'>
  <img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1441561110i/16123396._SY540_.png' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/>
</a><br/><br/><b>The Beginning:</b> <i>Two men were assigned to drop sarin gas on the Chiyda line: Ikuo Hayashi and Tomomitsu Niimi.</i><br/><br/>In ‘Underground’, Murakami interviews victims of the Tokyo gas attack. He asks them about their life and work, makes them describe in detail how they experienced the attack, what happened afterwards and how their life has been ever since.<br/><br/>It was a terrifying yet fascinating read. You could feel the claustrophobia and fear as people in the underground discovered that something was wrong,</span>
  <span id="freeText6843746385621380418" style="display:none"><a href="http://bookwormscloset.com/underground/" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1441561110i/16123396._SY540_.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br><b>The Beginning:</b> <i>Two men were assigned to drop sarin gas on the Chiyda line: Ikuo Hayashi and Tomomitsu Niimi.</i><br><br>In ‘Underground’, Murakami interviews victims of the Tokyo gas attack. He asks them about their life and work, makes them describe in detail how they experienced the attack, what happened afterwards and how their life has been ever since.<br><br>It was a terrifying yet fascinating read. You could feel the claustrophobia and fear as people in the underground discovered that something was wrong, and the panic when they started getting really sick and no help came. The most terrifying descriptions were of the victims who didn’t understand that they had been subject to a terror attack. Some were asleep on the train, and woke up confused and sick, just hurrying out and stumbled into work. Some were in complete shock and went on with their daily routine, even though they felt very sick and couldn’t see properly. It made me think how horrifying it must be to be subject to a terror attack. And how confusing it must be as well.<br><br>No one said a thing, everyone was so quiet. No response, no communication. I lived in America for a year, and believe me if the same thing had happened in America, there would have been a real scene. With everyone shouting “What’s going on here?” and coming together to find the cause.<br><br>My blog: <a href="http://bookwormscloset.com/underground/" rel="nofollow noopener">The Bookworm's Closet</a><br><br>Some reading moments with Underground:<br><a href="http://bookwormscloset.com/underground/" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1441561110i/16123397._SY540_.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br><a href="http://bookwormscloset.com/underground/" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1441561110i/16123398._SY540_.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a></span>

<div id="review_2430985230" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2430985230" />
    <a title="Rusalka" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6163718-rusalka"><img alt="Rusalka" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1591511894p2/6163718.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2430985230?book_show_action=true">Jan 17, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rusalka" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rusalka" href="/user/show/6163718-rusalka">Rusalka</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6163718-rusalka?shelf=japan">japan</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6163718-rusalka?shelf=around-the-world">around-the-world</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6163718-rusalka?shelf=ylto-group-theme-reads">ylto-group-theme-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6163718-rusalka?shelf=translation">translation</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6163718-rusalka?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6163718-rusalka?shelf=own">own</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2430985230" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8229965048447749489">I really love Murakami as a novelist. But he needs to stay as that. He is not a journalist. As the almost 7 months to read this book will attest.<br/><br/>Murakami takes a really interesting subject and makes it... well... boring. The repetitive nature of his interviews with survivors, which really shouldn't be. The preachy, pseudo-psych, pseudo-philosophy essay in the middle which is peppered with "I'm just a novelist but" which undermines him and makes him look arrogant at the same time. The best part o</span>
  <span id="freeText8229965048447749489" style="display:none">I really love Murakami as a novelist. But he needs to stay as that. He is not a journalist. As the almost 7 months to read this book will attest.<br /><br />Murakami takes a really interesting subject and makes it... well... boring. The repetitive nature of his interviews with survivors, which really shouldn't be. The preachy, pseudo-psych, pseudo-philosophy essay in the middle which is peppered with "I'm just a novelist but" which undermines him and makes him look arrogant at the same time. The best part of the book, and why I kept reading in case it was better, was the interviews with the ex-cult members. Murakami directs the interviews and challenges the interviewees more. And they portray different experiences to each other, which is actually interesting.<br /><br />And here is where I display my own bias. While I found the crimes horrifying, and the descriptions of the events terrible, I had huge trouble sympathising and understanding the victims from my own cultural biases. <br /><br />I cannot understand people who were in the middle of a medical/terrorist emergency walking or sometimes crawling to work out of an inbuilt obligation to do so, and possibly exposing coworkers to toxic gas as a result. I didn't get people watching the news in tearooms of their exact train, hearing descriptions of symptoms, going "well I can't breathe or can't see, but I'll keep on working!" instead of going to hospital. <br />I couldn't understand being on the train hours earlier to work as you had to work 3-4 hours a day for free to keep your job. I can't understand the appeal to authority that no emergency services took real control, or the fact that it was a vaguely rogue uni professor who got the word out that it was sarin and how to treat it, and that there was no comeuppance for the chaos or any lessons learnt from the event due to the deference to authority. <br />I cannot understand people who's lives are so regimented that they state which carriage of a train they get into every day and which exact seat they sit in. EVERY DAY. It blows my mind, and it is just so outside my experience, and shows why I could not as an Australian ever be a salaryman in Japan as much as I think it's a good idea some days.<br /><br />And that is good. I had a non-rose-coloured glasses look into Japanese life which I needed. Murakami states that Japan needed this too because of that appeal to authority, and that need to socially regulate everything to the chair on the train you sit on everyday, that they needed to examine those they let fester in their society as just viewing them as outside society and a bit odd. <br /><br />And while I respect and agree with all that, fuck, it was boring.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8229965048447749489" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3070121620" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer382155867243898960">Utterly fascinating, gripping and somehow tender, non-sensationalist collection of the author's interviews with various people injured and otherwise affected by the Tokyo subway sarin gas attacks. In an addition to the original, the author has included interviews with members and former members of Aum Shirinkiyo, the cult that perpetrated the attacks. Oddly I find these less compelling than the original interviews - in some ways they lapse into a dreary deluded sameness rather than shedding ligh</span>
  <span id="freeText382155867243898960" style="display:none">Utterly fascinating, gripping and somehow tender, non-sensationalist collection of the author's interviews with various people injured and otherwise affected by the Tokyo subway sarin gas attacks. In an addition to the original, the author has included interviews with members and former members of Aum Shirinkiyo, the cult that perpetrated the attacks. Oddly I find these less compelling than the original interviews - in some ways they lapse into a dreary deluded sameness rather than shedding light onto the cult's motives - but the book as a whole is excellent.</span>

<div id="review_303543786" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/303543786" />
    <a title="Aryn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6415628-aryn"><img alt="Aryn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1560285805p2/6415628.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/303543786?book_show_action=true">Mar 30, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Aryn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Aryn" href="/user/show/6415628-aryn">Aryn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780375725807" href="/book/show/229173.Underground">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6415628-aryn?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6415628-aryn?shelf=biography">biography</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6415628-aryn?shelf=owned-by-evan">owned-by-evan</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6415628-aryn?shelf=history">history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6415628-aryn?shelf=short-story">short-story</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6415628-aryn?shelf=multicultural">multicultural</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer303543786" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6667618686679904782">On <b>March 20</b>, 1995 a Japanese religious cult, called Aum Shinrikyo released sarin gas onto five subway trains during the morning rush hour. Cult members entered trains near the front with two or three newspaper-wrapped packets of sarin, piercing the packets with <b>sharpened umbrellas</b> the members were able to get off the train with minimal injury due to the gas.<br/><br/>In Japan, this book was published as two: the first being interviews with sarin survivors that had been affected in some way, even just havi</span>
  <span id="freeText6667618686679904782" style="display:none">On <b>March 20</b>, 1995 a Japanese religious cult, called Aum Shinrikyo released sarin gas onto five subway trains during the morning rush hour. Cult members entered trains near the front with two or three newspaper-wrapped packets of sarin, piercing the packets with <b>sharpened umbrellas</b> the members were able to get off the train with minimal injury due to the gas.<br /><br />In Japan, this book was published as two: the first being interviews with sarin survivors that had been affected in some way, even just having their day disrupted; the second being interviews with members and ex-members of Aum that agreed to be interviewed. The first part of the book definitely deserves <b>five stars</b>. The second part of the book I found less intriguing, which surprised me. Maybe it's the way that the Aum talk about enlightenment that I really just had trouble with because it's so foreign to my <b>western</b> thought processes.<br /><br />My lack of knowledge of this attack is pretty disgusting. But the thing is, that even spell check didn't recognize "<b>sarin</b>." Leads me to believe it's probably not just me that doesn't know enough about the attack. Hell, I didn't even know what sarin <i>was</i> or <i>did</i> until the last interviews in the first part of this book which were interviews with doctors who treated the sarin. The only thing I really knew about sarin, I learned from the introduction to the book, in a foot note.<br /><blockquote>
  <i>Sarin is a nerve gas invented by German scientists in the 1930s as part of Adolf Hitler's preparations for World War II. During the 1980s it was used to lethal effects by Iraq, both in the war against Iran and against the Kurds. Twenty-six times as deadly as cyanide gas, a drop of sarin the size of a pinhead is sufficient to kill a person.</i>
</blockquote><br />In the interview with the doctors, the most helpful part was this:<br /><blockquote>
  <i>If you want to move a suckle the nerve endings send out an order to the muscle cells in the form of the chemical, acetylcholine. It's the messenger. When the muscle receives that they move, they contract. After the contraction, the enzyme cholinestrerase serves to neutralize the message sent by the acetylcholine, which prepares for the next action. Over and over again.<br /><br />However when the cholinestrerase runs out, the acetylcholine message remains active and the muscle stays contracted. [...] when they stay contracted we get paralysis.</i>
</blockquote><br /><br />As I mentioned earlier, I enjoyed the first half of this book quite a bit more than the second half. What was really striking was how <b>surreal</b> the event felt to the people who were involved. None of them really seemed to know what was going on or how to handle it. Even if they did hear that it <b>might be sarin gas</b>, the the overwhelming response seemed to be: "well, it couldn't be me, better be off or else I'll be late for work!"<br /><br />My favorite interviews were the ones with <b>Tatsuo and Shizuko Akashi</b>. Shizuko was on Marunouchi Line, towards Ogikubo. She was the one most affected by sarin that agreed to be interviewed for Murakami's book. She lost much of her memory, ability to speak and walk. At the time this book was published she was in the hospital doing therapies to attempt to help her regain her life. The two interviews felt, to me, to be the two most emotional, and therefore resonated best with me.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6667618686679904782" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_681976657" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/681976657" />
    <a title="Abhinav" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10051075-abhinav"><img alt="Abhinav" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1388563530p2/10051075.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/681976657?book_show_action=true">Jul 31, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Abhinav" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Abhinav" href="/user/show/10051075-abhinav">Abhinav</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="ebook 9781448103720" href="/book/show/17430814-underground">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10051075-abhinav?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10051075-abhinav?shelf=found-in-translation">found-in-translation</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10051075-abhinav?shelf=2014-reading-saga">2014-reading-saga</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10051075-abhinav?shelf=the-cj-library">the-cj-library</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10051075-abhinav?shelf=i-own-an-ebook">i-own-an-ebook</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10051075-abhinav?shelf=true-crime">true-crime</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10051075-abhinav?shelf=semi-favorites">semi-favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer681976657" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1973561170424707270">I still remember the first time I came across this book at the local used-book store. I was about to check out with my purchases when I noticed a book with Murakami written on its spine behind the woman at the counter, so I asked her for it & quickly looked up the book on Goodreads. But I wasn't really keen on non-fiction back then, someone on my friends' list had given it a one-star rating & I being short on cash conspired together & I didn't buy it. Bad judgement on my part, now that I think a</span>
  <span id="freeText1973561170424707270" style="display:none">I still remember the first time I came across this book at the local used-book store. I was about to check out with my purchases when I noticed a book with Murakami written on its spine behind the woman at the counter, so I asked her for it &amp; quickly looked up the book on Goodreads. But I wasn't really keen on non-fiction back then, someone on my friends' list had given it a one-star rating &amp; I being short on cash conspired together &amp; I didn't buy it. Bad judgement on my part, now that I think about it.<br /><br />"Underground" is partly an interviewee memoir &amp; partly a work of investigative journalism, narrated in Rashomon-style from the viewpoints of people affected in the Tokyo Subway Gas Attack. At the very start, Murakami makes it clear that the biggest motivation behind writing this book was to search for the answer to the one question that the media reports never really bothered to find out - "What actually happened in the subway on March 20, 1995?"<br /><br />Despite Murakami acknowledging that he isn't a great verbal communicator, he does well to steer clear of stating his own involvement in the interviews &amp; mentions himself as little as possible, limiting his inputs to a few occasional clarifications or comments &amp; showing the sensitivity required to handle such a delicate matter. This helps the victim to really open up during the interview, speaking at length about themselves (including digressions at times) &amp; recounting their horrific experiences of that fateful Monday. Even though it feels a tad repetitive, what engages the reader is the insight these experiences provide into how the Japanese mindset works. Murakami does correctly point out that though these accounts of victims are after all just human memories &amp; not exactly hard facts, they often prove more enlightening than consistency probably ever would.<br /><br />The book, when originally published, included only the accounts of the victims. Murakami, however, paid heed to some sections of his readership who suggested that he also should've interviewed some members of Aum Shinrikyo - the religious cult who perpetrated the attack - to give a glimpse of both sides of the story. Though he appears a bit prejudiced &amp; even harsh at times (my observation entirely) towards the members of Aum (all of them here had no hand in the gas attack), it probably stems from the amount of damage &amp; hurt he'd experienced first-hand while interacting with the victims before. But then, Murakami is not a professional journalist &amp; he is more likely to express himself than any stoic journo would.<br /><br />"Underground" by Haruki Murakami is a riveting work of investigative reportage &amp; certainly succeeds in achieving what it sets out to do - try to give an unbiased account of both sides of the Tokyo Gas Attack &amp; explore where the Japanese as a responsible society is going off the rails. Highly recommended.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1973561170424707270" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_556525359" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/556525359" />
    <a title="Cărăşălu" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5885579-c-r-lu"><img alt="Cărăşălu" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1345808900p2/5885579.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/556525359?book_show_action=true">Mar 09, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cărăşălu" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cărăşălu" href="/user/show/5885579-c-r-lu">Cărăşălu</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="ebook 9780307762757" href="/book/show/9062440-underground">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer556525359" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1985055447206650952">This a collection of interviews with the victims of the Tokyo subway gas attack, in the first part, and with some (ex)members of the cult that did carry out the attack. Although I did find the latter part more interesting, the first one is very revealing as well. Murakami sets out to show how the event was felt by those directly involved in it. What exactly happened that very morning? What did people do? Who reacted how? And the answers are very surprising, at times unbelievable. One of the most</span>
  <span id="freeText1985055447206650952" style="display:none">This a collection of interviews with the victims of the Tokyo subway gas attack, in the first part, and with some (ex)members of the cult that did carry out the attack. Although I did find the latter part more interesting, the first one is very revealing as well. Murakami sets out to show how the event was felt by those directly involved in it. What exactly happened that very morning? What did people do? Who reacted how? And the answers are very surprising, at times unbelievable. One of the most recurrent themes is that people unaware of what had happened, but affected by the gas, were still trying to get to work. Almost blinded by the sarin, barely walking, they simply had to get to work. Some fainted on the way, others reached their workplaces, but were then sent to hospitals. Also, it is fascinating the way they tried to explain their state through the knowledge they had at hand: some though it was because of a recent flu, or because some medicine, or because a diet and so on. Very few did make a connection between the weird smell in the subway and their condition. <br /><br />In the second part, Murakami manages to find some members of Aum, the cult that carried out the attack, some of them having left Aum and others still sticking with it. The things they say, in a certain way, seem even more incredible than those in the first part. The way they accepted to do so many things, the way obedience and work for the cult made them happy, the effects of yoga training, their relationship with their Selves and with the Master, the practical application of a very distorted form of Buddhism, are so many thought-provoking things. Besides the inner workings of a sect, these interviews show major problems and faults in the outside society.<br /><br />All in all, a very valuable documents, especially from a sociological and psychological point of view, but worth reading for anyone interested in understanding what the hell modernity is doing to people.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1985055447206650952" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_80471617" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/80471617" />
    <a title="Meg - A Bookish Affair" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1132408-meg---a-bookish-affair"><img alt="Meg - A Bookish Affair" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1312494581p2/1132408.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/80471617?book_show_action=true">Dec 09, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Meg - A Bookish Affair" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Meg - A Bookish Affair" href="/user/show/1132408-meg---a-bookish-affair">Meg - A Bookish Affair</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1132408-meg---a-bookish-affair?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1132408-meg---a-bookish-affair?shelf=2015">2015</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer80471617" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8461514313965821246">Before reading "Underground," I had never read any non-fiction from Haruki Murakami. His fiction books are some of my favorites and if you know Murakami, you know that magical realism is part of so many of his fiction books so I was interested to see how his non-fiction was where there wouldn't be any magical realism.<br/><br/>This book is about the sarin gas attacks that happened in Tokyo in the mid-nineties. I vaguely remember when this happened but was quite young so I didn't really understand what had</span>
  <span id="freeText8461514313965821246" style="display:none">Before reading "Underground," I had never read any non-fiction from Haruki Murakami. His fiction books are some of my favorites and if you know Murakami, you know that magical realism is part of so many of his fiction books so I was interested to see how his non-fiction was where there wouldn't be any magical realism.<br /><br />This book is about the sarin gas attacks that happened in Tokyo in the mid-nineties. I vaguely remember when this happened but was quite young so I didn't really understand what had happened. As a frequent rider of public transportation now, this event still makes me quite fearful. As Murakami points out, it is still very much on the minds of the Japanese people as well. Murakami interviews various people that were affected in the gas attack. He also interviews some of the cult members and former cult members that carried out this horrendous event. These people come from all walks of life and were affected in different ways, which really gave me a well-rounded perspective of what the event meant as a whole to the populace of Japan. <br /><br />Even after the event occurred, so many of the victims faced issues. Many of them were affected by the gas and it changed the way they were able to function and interact with the world. I was struck by the lack of understanding that the victims faced from those around them who were not directly affected by the attacks. Murakami looks at this event from all angles to help readers understand the human side of this event. A good read!</span>
  <a data-text-id="8461514313965821246" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_107118912" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/107118912" />
    <a title="Chelsea Szendi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3675222-chelsea-szendi"><img alt="Chelsea Szendi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1333460966p2/3675222.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/107118912?book_show_action=true">Jun 14, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chelsea Szendi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chelsea Szendi" href="/user/show/3675222-chelsea-szendi">Chelsea Szendi</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3675222-chelsea-szendi?shelf=current-events">current-events</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3675222-chelsea-szendi?shelf=japan">japan</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer107118912" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer776876162853926840">This book proves two things I've long suspected about Murakami Haruki. One is that he'd be a lot more interesting if he'd deny his own ego every once in a while, and the second is that he is not very skilled at analysis. This book of Murakami's is nonfiction and almost entirely the compiled testimonies of survivors of the March 20, 1995 sarin attack on the Tokyo subway.<br/><br/>At first the experiment is absolutely absorbing. In his prologue, Murakami promises to explore the kind of "double victimization</span>
  <span id="freeText776876162853926840" style="display:none">This book proves two things I've long suspected about Murakami Haruki. One is that he'd be a lot more interesting if he'd deny his own ego every once in a while, and the second is that he is not very skilled at analysis. This book of Murakami's is nonfiction and almost entirely the compiled testimonies of survivors of the March 20, 1995 sarin attack on the Tokyo subway.<br /><br />At first the experiment is absolutely absorbing. In his prologue, Murakami promises to explore the kind of "double victimization" that often occurs in Japanese society, when victims of circumstances beyond their control are further victimized by colleagues and neighbors because their misfortune makes them different. I can think of many examples beyond the Aum attack - from hibakusha to Minamata. The short chapters themselves, however, are mostly descriptions of the victims' movements on March 20, or their symptoms.<br /><br />The subtitle of the book is "The Tokyo Gas Attack and the Japanese Psyche." It's kind of cringe-worthy to throw out that term - the Japanese psyche - but I began to wish there was more synthesis of people's stories and analyses of patterns. Ironically, by the time I reached the kind of postscript inclusion of narratives offered by Aum Shinrikyo participants I felt that I understood the social alienation many of them described; so many of the victims' stories seemed to reveal a kind of automaton existence in an enormous and estranging city. </span>
  <a data-text-id="776876162853926840" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1173338301" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1173338301" />
    <a title="G." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5487874-g"><img alt="G." src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1456934593p2/5487874.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1173338301?book_show_action=true">Jan 19, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="G." class="user" itemprop="url" name="G." href="/user/show/5487874-g">G.</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780375703881" href="/book/show/15225517-underground">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1173338301" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14321317998594821797">3.5/5<br/><br/><i>Underground</i> is Murakami's way of "figuring" out the 1995 Tokyo sarin gas attack. The way he decided to do this was by first interviewing some of the survivors and, in some cases, the relatives of the deceased, and second - just to give us a glimpse of the other side of the coin - interviewing some of the Aum cult followers. There was a brief Murakami piece - I struggle calling it an analysis - between the two sets of interviews. This brief interlude in the middle is, in my opinion, the weak</span>
  <span id="freeText14321317998594821797" style="display:none">3.5/5<br /><br /><i>Underground</i> is Murakami's way of "figuring" out the 1995 Tokyo sarin gas attack. The way he decided to do this was by first interviewing some of the survivors and, in some cases, the relatives of the deceased, and second - just to give us a glimpse of the other side of the coin - interviewing some of the Aum cult followers. There was a brief Murakami piece - I struggle calling it an analysis - between the two sets of interviews. This brief interlude in the middle is, in my opinion, the weakest part of the book. Murakami is very much a fiction writer, but he's not very good at presenting a non-fiction analysis. However, the book is worth reading for the interviews alone. They paint a vivid picture of March 20th 1995, give us a glimpse of the aftermath of a terrible act, provide us with some insight into how Aum worked. Beside being a sort of chronicle of a horrific event, <i>Underground</i> lets us glean some insight into Japanese culture. All in all, I'd say <i>Underground</i> is well worth reading. </span>
  <a data-text-id="14321317998594821797" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
